Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Defining 'Shart' and the Purities Required for Prayer
Defining 'shart' linguistically and legally, then listing the conditions of prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'shart' in Hanafi legal terminology and state the four purities that are conditions for the validity of prayer, with their proofs from Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
A condition is an external prerequisite not part of the act; prayer has four purification conditions.

This lesson opens the chapter on the conditions of prayer from Al-Bahr al-Raiq. The author first clarifies the linguistic and legal meaning of 'shart', distinguishing it from cause and sign. He then states the four purities that must precede prayer: purity of the body from both ritual impurity (hadath) and physical impurity (khabath), and purity of the garment and the place. Understanding these conditions is essential because prayer is invalid if any is missing.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of shart
Identify the linguistic meaning (sign) and legal definition (external prerequisite). Note the Hanafi distinction between shart, sabab, 'illa, and 'alamah.
Legal definition: that upon which existence depends without being part of the essence.
2
Distinguish types of shart
Real vs. artificial; legal vs. non-legal. Recognize that conditions of prayer belong to the legal artificial type.
Shart shar'i: legally required (e.g., purity for prayer).
3
List the four conditions of prayer
Purity of body from hadath, body from khabath, garment, place. Why are these conditions? Because prayer depends on them externally.
Conditions are not arkan; they are external prerequisites.
4
Examine the proofs for each condition
For hadath: verses on wudu/ghusl. For khabath: hadith on urine and Fatima's hadith. For garment: Quran 74:4. For place: by primary reasoning (awla).
The imperative 'tahhir' in 74:4 indicates obligation.
5
Note the discussion on precedence of hadath
Why did the author give precedence to hadath over khabath? Understand the argument that hadath is indivisible and its small amount prevents prayer, unlike khabath. Also note the rebuttal from Ghayat al-Bayan.
The precedence is not a legal distinction but a descriptive one.

The Book of Prayer - Chapter on Conditions of Prayer. Diya' al-Hulum: an abridgment of Shams al-'Ulum on lexicography. Whoever here uses 'shara'it' has contradicted the language as you know, and the morphological rule, for 'fa'a'il' is not recorded as a plural of a word with pattern fa'l (with fatha on fa and sukun on 'ayn), unlike the expression because its singular is faridah like saha'if plural of sahifah; and it means in language 'sign'. Likewise 'fara'id' is correct in Fath al-Qadir. As for in al-Sihah: 'al-shart' is known, and 'al-shart' with tashrik means 'sign'; and His saying 'indeed the signs of it have come' [Muhammad:18] means its signs. In the Shari'ah: that upon which the existence of something depends and is not part of it. And the usuliyyun have divided the external matter related to the ruling into that which has influence and that which leads to it without influence. The first is 'illa (cause), the second is sabab (reason). Otherwise, if existence depends on it, it is a condition (shart); otherwise, if it indicates it, it is a sign ('alamah). And the condition is real and artificial. The first: that upon which something depends in reality. The second: legal (shar'i) i.e. by command of the Lawgiver, so it is legally dependent, such as witnesses for marriage and purity for prayer; and non-legal, i.e. by the command of the legally responsible person by suspending his action on it with the permission of the Lawgiver, like 'if you enter the house, such-and-such'. And al-Shumni mentioned that the intended meaning of 'conditions' here is that by which the legally responsible person does not become starting the prayer, cautioning against the takbir (tahrimah), for it is a condition according to us and is not mentioned in this chapter. End. And he did not restrict the conditions with precedence as in the Mukhtasar, because there is no need, since they are a descriptive characteristic, not a specifier, for the condition is only preceding. And what the commentators (sharihun) have mentioned contrary to that, he has refuted it in Fath al-Qadir. His saying: (they are purity of his body from hadath and khabath, and his garment and his place). As for purity of his body, it is by the verse of wudu and ghusl. And from khabath, by his saying: 'Avoid urine, for the general punishment of the grave is because of it?' and by the hadith of Fatima bint Abi Hubaysh: 'Wash the blood from you and pray'. And hadath is a legal preventative (mani'iyyah shar'iyyah) established in the limbs until the use of the remover, and khabath is an impurity considered unclean by the Shari'ah. And he gave precedence to hadath because of its strength, for a small amount of it prevents, contrary to a small amount of khabath. And in Ghayat al-Bayan: 'And in it there is consideration, because a drop of wine or blood or urine if it falls into a well it becomes impure, while a junub or one with hadath if he inserts his hand into a utensil it does not become impure. And the better view is to say: there is no precedence in it, because the waw is for absolute conjunction. End. And earlier in the chapter on impurities some of this has been discussed.' And as for purity of his garment, it is by His saying 'And your garment purify' [al-Muddaththir:4], for the most apparent view is that the intended meaning is your garments that you wear, and its meaning is to purify them from impurity. And other interpretations of the verse have been stated, but the most weighty is what we have mentioned, and it is the saying of the jurists, and it is correct as al-Nawawi mentioned in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab, and by the generality of the two previous hadiths. And when purification is obligatory for what we have mentioned concerning the garment, it is even more necessary for the place and the body, because they are more binding for the pray-er than it, due to its conceivability of separation unlike them. And by khabath he intended that which prevents, which he had preceded in the chapter on impurities, so the general expression does not apply. And he indicated by stipulating purity of the garment that if he carries a preventive impurity, his prayer is invalid. Similarly, if the impurity is in the end of his turban or his handkerchief which is intended as his garment that he is wearing, and he throws that end on the ground and prays, then if it moves with his movement it is not permissible, otherwise it is permissible, because by that movement it is attributed to carrying impurity. And in al-Zahiriyyah: 'If a boy has his garment impure or himself impure and sits on the stone of the pray-er while he is leaning, or if an impure dove[?] falls on the head of the pray-er and it is... otherwise it would be a cause ('illa) and not leading in general, otherwise it would be a sabab. And what is in Ghurar al-Adhkar that the condition of a thing is that which exists when that thing exists, not by its existence nor without it altogether. Sabab. His saying: 'And what the commentators mentioned...' he said in Fath al-Qadir: 'This is the actual explanation. And it is said to exclude the rational condition (like life for pain) and the artificial (like entering the house for divorce). And it is said to exclude what does not precede it, like sitting as a condition for exiting and repeating what has not been prescribed as a condition for remaining in validity. On the second view, the condition, whether rational or otherwise, is preceding, so the restriction of precedence does not exclude the rational and artificial, because the precedence of life and entering the house is certain, for example over pain and the occurrence of divorce. It cannot be said that the artificial is for the occurrence of the suspended, for the condition has no effect except in the opposite, so shart is that upon which other depends without its having any effect in it, except that it is called shart in language. But we deny it. Rather the sabab is his saying 'you are divorced'; its operation is delayed until the existence of the artificial condition, so it is true that it depends on it without being an effect in it. So the first view is determined. And because his saying 'which precede it' is a restriction on the conditions of prayer, not on the absolute condition, and there is no artificial condition in prayer. And it is unlikely to exclude its rational condition like life and the like, because the book was written to explain practical matters, so it does not consider other things. And the conditions of exiting and remaining in validity are not conditions for prayer but for another matter, namely exiting and remaining. And it is permissible to say 'condition of prayer' by a kind of metaphorical extension of the name of the whole to the part and to the adjacent description.' His saying: 'And he gave precedence to hadath because of its strength, because a little of it prevents' there is consideration, because hadath has no littleness, because it does not divide, and it is possible that by 'a little of it' he meant a speck, leniently. And what was objected in Ghayat al-Bayan is not applicable to the correct view of the purity of used water, and on the view of its impurity, the response is that what is meant by 'more severe' is severity in terms of preventing prayer, said by some virtuous ones. His saying: 'The intended garment is that which he is wearing' - this was inserted in the middle of the speech to indicate that the intended meaning is not specifically the handkerchief but more general.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq covers the beginning of the chapter on conditions of prayer from Kanz al-Daqaiq. It opens with a detailed lexicographical and legal definition of the term 'شرط' (shart/condition). The author notes that 'شرائط' (shara'it) is not the standard plural for 'شرط' (with sukun on shin); the correct plural is 'أشراط' (ashrat) meaning signs. In legal terminology, a condition is defined as 'that upon which the existence of something depends without being part of its essence.' The usul scholars distinguish conditions from causes ('illah), means (sabab), and signs ('alamah). Conditions are further divided into real (haqiqi) and artificial (ja'li), with the latter being either legal (shar'i, like purity for prayer) or conventional (like suspending divorce on entry). The author then quotes Al-Shumni clarifying that the 'conditions' in this chapter refer to what must be present before one starts prayer, excluding the takbir (tahrimah) which is also a condition but not mentioned here. The main matn states the conditions: purity of the body from both hadath (ritual impurity) and khabath (physical impurity), and purity of garment and place. The commentary provides proofs: purity from hadath is from the verses of wudu and ghusl; purity from khabath from the hadith about avoiding urine and the hadith of Fatima; purity of garment from the verse 'wa thiyabaka fathhir'. The discussion includes a preference for giving precedence to hadath due to its strength (a small amount prevents prayer, unlike a small amount of khabath), though this is contested because hadath is indivisible. The author also addresses nuances about impurity on a garment part, and the obligation of purity for place and body by primary reasoning (awla). The passage concludes with a return to the definition of 'shart', arguing that the restriction of precedence applies specifically to the conditions of prayer, not to conditions in general.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): That upon which the existence of something depends without being part of its essence.
حدث (hadath): A legal preventative (mani'iyyah shar'iyyah) residing in the limbs until purification is performed.
خبث (khabath): A physically impure substance considered unclean by the Shari'ah (najasah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal definition of 'shart'?
A) The effective cause ('illa) of a ruling
B) Something external on which the existence of something depends without being part of it
C) A sign ('alamah) indicating a ruling
D) A means (sabab) that leads to a ruling

Key Takeaway
Purity from hadath, khabath on body, garment, and place are all essential conditions for prayer; without any one, the prayer is invalid.

Small Hadath vs Small Khabath: The Preventer Distinction
Commentary on the ruling that small hadath is a preventer, unlike small khabath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on small hadath and small khabath and apply the distinction to determine whether an impurity prevents valid prayer.

Core Idea
Any hadath prevents prayer; small khabath (less than a dirham) does not.

This lesson examines a crucial distinction in Hanafi taharah: a small amount of ritual impurity (hadath) invalidates wudu and thus prevents prayer, whereas a small amount of physical impurity (khabath) on the body or clothes is excused if it is less than the size of a dirham. The commentator also resolves an apparent contradiction regarding water impurity, clarifying that the original text's conjunction does not imply a logical contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of impurity
Recognize that hadath (ritual impurity) involves things that nullify wudu, while khabath (physical impurity) includes najasat like blood, urine, or wine.
This distinction is foundational for understanding their different rules.
2
Apply the ruling to each type
For hadath: any amount (even a small drop of urine exiting the body) breaks wudu and prevents prayer until wudu is renewed. For khabath: a small amount (less than a dirham) is excused and does not invalidate prayer.
The Hanafi school differs from some others in excusing small physical impurities.
3
Understand the commentary's resolution
The commentator refutes a potential objection from Ghayat al-Bayan that a drop of impurity in a well makes water impure, while a junub's hand in water does not. He explains the original matn's word 'wa' (and) does not imply precedence; the two cases are independent.
This highlights careful linguistic analysis in fiqh commentaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قليله مانع بخلاف قليل الخبث. وفي غاية البيان وفيه نظر لأن القطرة من الخمر أو الدم أو البول إذا وقعت في البئر تنجس والجنب أو المحدث إذا أدخل يده في الإناء لا ينجس . والأولى أن يقال : ليس فيه تقديم لأن الواو لمطلق الجمع اهـ. وقد تقدم في الأنجاس شيء منه .
A small amount of it (hadath) is a preventer, unlike a small amount of physical impurity (khabath). And in Ghayat al-Bayan there is a consideration because a drop of wine, blood, or urine if it falls into a well, it becomes impure, while a junub or one with hadath if he inserts his hand into a container, it does not become impure. And the better approach is to say: There is no precedence in it because the waw (و) is for absolute conjunction, end quote. And something of this has preceded in the chapter on impurities.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage comments on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq, explaining that a small amount of hadath (ritual impurity) prevents valid prayer because it nullifies wudu, whereas a small amount of khabath (physical impurity) like a drop of blood does not prevent if it is less than the amount of a dirham. The author critiques a statement from Ghayat al-Bayan that seemed to suggest a contradiction: why does a drop of impurity in water make it impure, but a junub’s hand in water does not? He resolves that there is no contradiction; the original pronouncement uses the conjunction 'wa' which does not imply sequence or preference. This topic was already discussed in the chapter on impurities.

Key Terms
حدث (hadath): Ritual impurity that nullifies wudu, e.g., passing wind, urine, etc.
خبث (khabath): Physical impurity (najis), e.g., blood, urine, alcohol.
قليل (qalil): A small amount; for hadath, any amount invalidates; for khabath, an amount less than a dirham is excused.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a small amount of hadath compared to a small amount of khabath?
A) Both are excused.
B) Small hadath is a preventer, small khabath is not.
C) Small khabath is a preventer, small hadath is not.
D) Both are preventers.

Key Takeaway
Small hadath invalidates wudu and prevents prayer; small khabath is excused and does not prevent prayer in the Hanafi school.

The Quranic Obligation of Purifying Garments from Khabath
Quranic proof for purification from physical impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Quranic proof from Surah al-Muddaththir (74:4) for the obligation of purity from physical impurity for prayer, and apply the a fortiori analogy to extend it to the body and place.

Core Idea
The command 'purify your garments' mandates removal of physical impurity; by stronger reason, the body and place must also be pure.

This lesson examines the primary textual evidence for the obligation of taharah from khabath. The Quranic verse 'And your garment purify' is interpreted by the Hanafi school as a direct command to physically cleanse one's clothing from najasah. By a fortiori reasoning (qiyas awla), this obligation necessarily extends to the body and the place of prayer, as they are more intimately connected to the worshiper.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic proof
Locate the verse (74:4) in the passage and note the imperative 'طهر'.
2
Analyze the interpretation
Understand that the most apparent meaning (zahir) is physical purification, not metaphorical.
The commentator cites al-Nawawi and the consensus of jurists.
3
Apply the a fortiori analogy
Explain why the obligation on the garment implies obligation on the body and place by stronger reason.
The garment can be removed, but body and place cannot, so they are more binding.
4
State the ruling
Conclude that taharah from khabath is required for the garment, body, and place of prayer according to the Hanafi school.
This is a condition (shart) for the validity of prayer.

وأما طهارة ثوبه فلقوله تعالى وثيابك فطهر ) [المدثر : ٤] فإن الأظهر أن المراد ثيابك الملبوسة وأن معناه طهرها من النجاسة. وقد قيل في الآية غير هذا لكن الأرجح ما ذكرناه وهو قول الفقهاء وهو الصحيح كما ذكره النووي في شرح المهذب، ولعموم الحديثين السابقين. وإذا وجب التطهير لما ذكرناه في الثوب وجب في المكان والبدن بالأولى لأنهما ألزم للمصلي منه لتصور انفصاله بخلافهما وأراد بالخبث القدر المانع الذي قدمه في باب الأنجاس فلا يرد عليه الإطلاق. وأشار باشتراط طهارة الثوب إلى أنه لو حمل نجاسة مانعة فإن صلاته باطلة. فكذا لو كانت النجاسة في طرف عمامته أو منديله المقصود ثوب هو لابسه فألقى ذلك الطرف على الأرض وصلى فإنه إن تحرك بحركته لا يجوز وإلا يجوز، لأنه بتلك الحركة ينسب لحمل النجاسة. وفي الظهيرية : الصبي إذا كان ثوبه نجساً أو هو نجس فجلس على حجر المصلي وهو يستمسك، أو الحمام النجس إذا وقع على رأس المصلي وهو وإلا كان علة وغير موصل في الجملة وإلا كان سبباً. وما في غرر الأذكار من أن شرط الشيء ما يوجد ذلك الشيء عند وجوده لا بوجوده ولا بدونه أجمع.

قوله: (وقدم الحدث لقوته لأن قليله مانع (الخ فيه نظر لأن الحدث لا قليل له لأنه لا يتجزأ، ويمكن أن يراد بقليله اللمعة تساهلاً، وما أورده في غاية البيان غير وارد على الصحيح من طهارة المستعمل، وعلى القول بنجاسته يجاب بأن المراد بالأغلظية الأغلظية من حيث منع الصلاة قاله بعض الفضلاء. قوله : (المقصود ثوب هو لابسه أقحم ذلك في أثناء الكلام لبيان أن المراد ليس خصوص المنديل بل أعم.

فلا اهـ .
يصلي كذلك جازت الصلاة، وكذلك الجنب أو المحدث إذا حمله المصلي لأن الذي على المصلي مستعمل له فلم يصر المصلي حاملاً للنجاسة ا.هـ. ودل كلامه أنه لو صلى ورأسه يصل إلى السقف النجس أو في كلة متنجسة أو في خيمة كذلك فإنها لا تصح لكونه حاملاً للنجاسة، ولهذا قال في القنية : إذا صلى في الخيمة ورفع سقفها لتمام قيامه جاز إذا كانت طاهرة وإلا وفي المحيط : لو صلى وفي يده حبل مشدود على عنق الكلب تجوز صلاته لأن الحبل لما سقط على الأرض فقد انقطع حكم الاتصال به فصار كالعمامة الطويلة ا هـ. وكذا لو كان الحبل مشدوداً في وسطه، وكذا لو كان مربوطاً في سفينة فيها نجاسة، ومذهب الشافعي أن الصلاة لا تصح في هذه المسائل لأنه حامل للنجاسة كما نقله النووي ولو صلى ومعه جرو كلب أو كل ما لا يجوز أن يتوضأ بسؤره ؛ قيل لم يجز والأصح أنه إن كان فمه مفتوحاً لم يجز لأن لعابه يسيل في كمه فيصير مبتلاً بلعابه فيتنجس كمه فيمنع الجواز إن كان أكثر من قدر الدرهم وإن كان فمه مشدوداً بحيث لا يصل لعابه إلى ثوبه جاز لأن ظاهر كل حيوان طاهر، ولا ينجس إلا بالموت ونجاسة باطنه في معدنه فلا يظهر حكمها كنجاسة باطن المصلي. ولو صلى وفي كمه قارورة مضمومة فيها بول لم تجز صلاته لأنه في غير معدنه ومكانه، ولو صلى وفي كمه بيضة مذرة قد صار محها دماً جازت لأنه في معدنه، والشيء ما دام في معدنه لا يعطي له حكم النجاسة. الكل في المحيط. وأراد بالمكان موضع القدم والسجود فقط، أما طهارة موضع القدم فباتفاق الروايات بشرط أن يضعهما على النجاسة، أما إن رفع القدم التي موضعها نجس وصلى جاز. وإما طهارة موضع السجود ففي أصح الروايتين عن أبي حنيفة وهو قولهما، وأما إن كانت النجاسة في موضع يديه وركبتيه وحذاء إبطيه وصدره جازت صلاته لأن الوضع على النجاسة كلا ،وضع، والسجود على اليدين والركبتين غير واجب فكأنه لم يسجد عليها وهذا ظاهر الرواية. واختار أبو الليث أن صلاته تفسد وصححه في العيون ولو صلى على مكان طاهر إلا أنه إذا سجد تقع ثيابه على أرض

قوله : (وأراد بالمكان الخ) قال في النهر : ليس في كلامه ما يدل على اختصاص المكان بما ذكر، بل الظاهر الإطلاق فقد اختار الفقيه خلاف ظاهر الرواية وصححه في العيون وهو المناسب لإطلاق عامة المتون. وفي الخانية : وكذا لو كانت النجاسة في موضع السجود أو الركبتين أو اليدين يعني تجمع ولا يجعل كأنه لم يضع العضو على النجاسة، وهذا كما لو صلى رافعاً إحدى قدميه جازت صلاته، ولو وضع القدم على النجاسة لا تجوز ولا يجعل كأنه لم يضع .اهـ. وهو يفيد أن عدم اشتراط طهارة مكان اليدين أو الركبتين إذا لم يضعهما، أما إن وضعهما اشترطت فليحفظ هذا كذا في فتح القدير. وأقول : لو خرج ما في الخانية على رأي الفقيه لكان أظهر فتدبره .اهـ. هذا وفي منية المصلي ما نصه : ذكر شمس الأئمة السرخسي أنه إذا كانت النجاسة موضع الكفين والركبتين جازت صلاته . البحر الرائق ج ١/
And as for the purity of his garment, for His statement: 'And your garment purify' (74:4). The most apparent is that it means your worn garment, and its meaning is to purify it from impurity. It has been said otherwise, but the preponderant is what we mentioned, and it is the statement of the jurists, and it is correct as mentioned by al-Nawawi in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab, and due to the generality of the two previous hadiths. And when purification is obligatory for the garment as we mentioned, it is obligatory for the place and the body by a fortiori reasoning, because they are more binding for the worshiper than the garment, since the garment can be removed unlike them. And by khabath he means the preventing amount that he mentioned in the chapter on impurities, so the absolute does not apply. And by stipulating purity of the garment, he indicates that if he carries a preventing impurity, his prayer is invalid. So also if the impurity is on the end of his turban or his handkerchief, which is intended as a garment he is wearing, and he throws that end on the ground and prays, then if it moves with his movement it is not permissible, otherwise it is permissible, because by that movement it is attributed to carrying the impurity. In al-Zahiriyyah: If a child's garment is impure or he is impure and he sits on the lap of the worshiper while he is supporting him, or a pigeon impure if it lands on the worshiper's head... and if it is not, it would be a cause and not a medium overall, and if not, it would be a cause. And what is in Ghurar al-Adhkar that the condition of a thing is that the thing exists when it exists, not by its existence nor without it, all together.

His saying: (And he gave precedence to hadath due to its strength, because its small amount is a preventer) there is a consideration because hadath has no small amount, as it is indivisible. And it is possible that by 'its small amount' he meant a small spot (lum'a) as a leniency. And what was mentioned in Ghayat al-Bayan is not valid according to the correct view of the purity of used water, and on the view of its impurity, the answer is that what is meant by 'greater severity' is severity in terms of preventing prayer, as some scholars said. His saying: (The intended garment is what he is wearing) he inserted that in the middle of the speech to explain that the meaning is not specifically the handkerchief but general.

So end quote.

He prays likewise, the prayer is valid. And similarly, if a junub or one with hadath is carried by the worshiper, because what is on the worshiper is used by him, so the worshiper does not become a carrier of impurity. End quote. And his statement indicates that if he prays while his head reaches an impure ceiling, or in an impure mosquito net, or in an impure tent, then it is not valid because he is a carrier of impurity. For this reason he said in al-Qunyah: If he prays in a tent and raises its roof for the completeness of his standing, it is valid if it is pure; otherwise, no. And in al-Muhit: If he prays while in his hand is a rope tied to the neck of a dog, his prayer is valid, because when the rope fell to the ground, the ruling of connection was severed, so it becomes like a long turban. End quote. Similarly, if the rope is tied around its waist, and similarly if it is tied to a ship containing impurity. The school of al-Shafi'i is that prayer is not valid in these matters because he is a carrier of impurity, as al-Nawawi transmitted. And if he prays with a puppy dog or whatever it is not permissible to perform wudu with its leftover water; it is said it is not valid, and the most correct is that if its mouth is open, it is not valid because its saliva flows into his sleeve, so it becomes wet with its saliva, thus his sleeve becomes impure, preventing validity if it is more than the amount of a dirham. But if its mouth is tied such that its saliva does not reach his garment, it is valid, because the apparent of every animal is pure, and it only becomes impure by death, and its internal impurity is in its organ, so its ruling does not appear, like the impurity of the inside of the worshiper. And if he prays while in his sleeve is a closed bottle containing urine, his prayer is not valid, because it is not in its organ and place. And if he prays while in his sleeve is a rotten egg whose yolk has turned to blood, it is valid, because it is in its organ. And as long as something is in its organ, it is not given the ruling of impurity. All from al-Muhit. And he meant by place: the place of the foot and prostration only. As for the purity of the place of the foot, it is by agreement of the narrations, on condition that he places them on the impurity. But if he lifts the foot whose place is impure and prays, it is valid. And as for the purity of the place of prostration, according to the soundest of the two narrations from Abu Hanifa, and it is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And if the impurity is in the place of his hands, knees, armpits, and chest, his prayer is valid, because placing on the impurity is not a placement, and prostration on the hands and knees is not obligatory, so it is as if he did not prostrate on them. And this is the apparent narration. Abu al-Layth chose that his prayer is invalid, and it was corrected in al-Uyun. And if he prays on a pure place, but when he prostrates his clothes fall on impure ground.

His saying: (And he meant by place etc.) He said in al-Nahr: There is nothing in his speech that indicates restriction of place to what was mentioned; rather, the apparent is absolute. Al-Faqih chose contrary to the apparent narration and corrected it in al-Uyun, and it is appropriate for the absolute of most texts. And in al-Khaniyyah: Similarly, if the impurity is in the place of prostration, knees, or hands, meaning he gathers? and it is not considered as if he did not place the limb on the impurity. This is like if he prays lifting one of his feet, his prayer is valid, but if he places the foot on impurity, it is not valid and it is not considered as if he did not place. End quote. And it indicates that not requiring purity of the place of the hands or knees is when he does not place them; but if he places them, it is required. So keep this. This is in Fath al-Qadir. And I say: If the statement in al-Khaniyyah were based on the view of al-Faqih, it would be more apparent, so contemplate it. End quote. And in Munyat al-Musalli: Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentioned that if impurity is in the place of the palms and knees, his prayer is valid. Al-Bahr al-Raiq vol. 1.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the obligation of purity from physical impurity (khabath) for prayer. It begins with the Quranic proof from Surah al-Muddaththir (74:4) that one must purify his garments, which the jurists interpret literally as physical purification. By a fortiori analogy (qiyas awla), this obligation extends to the body and place of prayer. The commentator then discusses various scenarios: if the end of one's turban touches impure ground and moves with the worshiper, the prayer is invalid because he is considered to be carrying the impurity. If it does not move, it is permissible. He then discusses a child with impure clothes sitting on the worshiper's lap: the prayer is valid because the child is not being carried by the worshiper as a load? Actually the text says if the child is on his lap and he is supporting him, the prayer is valid. Similarly, carrying a junub or one with hadath does not invalidate prayer. Then he discusses a rope tied to a dog: if the rope falls to the ground, the connection is severed and prayer is valid. The Shafi'i school holds the opposite view. For a dog with an open mouth, the saliva may wet the sleeve and invalidate prayer if the amount of impurity exceeds a dirham. For a bottle of urine in the sleeve, prayer is invalid because the urine is not in its original place (the bladder). For a rotten egg whose yolk has turned to blood, prayer is valid because it is in its original place. He then discusses the place of prayer: the place of the feet and prostration must be pure. For the feet, if they are placed on impurity, prayer is invalid; if lifted, it is valid. For prostration, the soundest narration from Abu Hanifa, and the view of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), is that the place of prostration must be pure. For the hands, knees, etc., the apparent narration is that impurity in those places does not invalidate, because placing them is not obligatory. However, Abu al-Layth chose invalidation. The commentator then cites al-Nahr and al-Khaniyyah, and concludes that if one does not place the hands/knees on impurity, prayer is valid, but if he places them, it is invalid. He ends by quoting Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi that prayer is valid even if impurity is under the palms and knees.

Key Terms
خبث (khabath): Physical impurity (najis) that must be removed for prayer validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the most apparent (azhar) meaning of 'purify your garments'?
A) Purify your heart from sin.
B) Purify your clothes from physical impurity.
C) Purify your place of prayer.
D) Both A and B.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic command to purify garments is the primary evidence for the obligation of removing physical impurity before prayer; by stronger reason, this obligation extends to the body and place.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
10 min
When is Impurity Considered 'Carried'? Cases of Indirect Contact
Practical scenarios of indirect impurity contact

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when indirect contact with impurity invalidates prayer by applying the Hanafi rule of 'connection' (whether the impurity moves with the worshiper).

Core Idea
Impurity invalidates prayer only if the worshiper is considered to be carrying it; if the connection is severed, the prayer remains valid.

This lesson explores scenarios where impurity is not directly on the worshiper's body or garment but is attached through an intermediary (e.g., a turban end, a child on the lap, a rope tied to a dog). The key legal criterion is whether the impurity moves with the worshiper's movement. This principle derives from the general requirement of taharah and is refined by the Hanafi school through detailed case law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle of 'carrying' impurity
The starting point: prayer becomes invalid if the worshiper carries a preventing impurity (khabath mani').
The text first states this general rule.
2
Analyze the turban end case
If the end of the turban or a handkerchief touches impurity on the ground, it depends on whether it moves with the worshiper.
If it moves (e.g., swings) with his movement, he is considered to be carrying it; if not, it is like a separate object.
3
Examine the child on the lap case
If an impure child sits on the worshiper's lap, the prayer is valid because the child is being supported but not 'carried' as a burden.
Contrast: if the child were lifted, the ruling might differ.
4
Evaluate the rope-tied-to-dog case
If the worshiper holds a rope tied to a dog, and the rope falls to the ground, the connection is severed and prayer is valid.
The rope is treated like a long turban: once it drops, the impurity is no longer attached.
5
Differentiate from the Shafi'i view
The Shafi'i school generally invalidates prayer in these scenarios because they consider any indirect connection as carrying.
The Hanafi school uses the concept of 'catene' (connection) being effectively cut.

فلا اهـ .
يصلي كذلك جازت الصلاة، وكذلك الجنب أو المحدث إذا حمله المصلي لأن الذي على المصلي مستعمل له فلم يصر المصلي حاملاً للنجاسة ا.هـ. ودل كلامه أنه لو صلى ورأسه يصل إلى السقف النجس أو في كلة متنجسة أو في خيمة كذلك فإنها لا تصح لكونه حاملاً للنجاسة، ولهذا قال في القنية : إذا صلى في الخيمة ورفع سقفها لتمام قيامه جاز إذا كانت طاهرة وإلا وفي المحيط : لو صلى وفي يده حبل مشدود على عنق الكلب تجوز صلاته لأن الحبل لما سقط على الأرض فقد انقطع حكم الاتصال به فصار كالعمامة الطويلة ا هـ. وكذا لو كان الحبل مشدوداً في وسطه، وكذا لو كان مربوطاً في سفينة فيها نجاسة، ومذهب الشافعي أن الصلاة لا تصح في هذه المسائل لأنه حامل للنجاسة كما نقله النووي ولو صلى ومعه جرو كلب أو كل ما لا يجوز أن يتوضأ بسؤره ؛ قيل لم يجز والأصح أنه إن كان فمه مفتوحاً لم يجز لأن لعابه يسيل في كمه فيصير مبتلاً بلعابه فيتنجس كمه فيمنع الجواز إن كان أكثر من قدر الدرهم وإن كان فمه مشدوداً بحيث لا يصل لعابه إلى ثوبه جاز لأن ظاهر كل حيوان طاهر، ولا ينجس إلا بالموت ونجاسة باطنه في معدنه فلا يظهر حكمها كنجاسة باطن المصلي. ولو صلى وفي كمه قارورة مضمومة فيها بول لم تجز صلاته لأنه في غير معدنه ومكانه، ولو صلى وفي كمه بيضة مذرة قد صار محها دماً جازت لأنه في معدنه، والشيء ما دام في معدنه لا يعطي له حكم النجاسة. الكل في المحيط. وأراد بالمكان موضع القدم والسجود فقط، أما طهارة موضع القدم فباتفاق الروايات بشرط أن يضعهما على النجاسة، أما إن رفع القدم التي موضعها نجس وصلى جاز. وإما طهارة موضع السجود ففي أصح الروايتين عن أبي حنيفة وهو قولهما، وأما إن كانت النجاسة في موضع يديه وركبتيه وحذاء إبطيه وصدره جازت صلاته لأن الوضع على النجاسة كلا ،وضع، والسجود على اليدين والركبتين غير واجب فكأنه لم يسجد عليها وهذا ظاهر الرواية. واختار أبو الليث أن صلاته تفسد وصححه في العيون ولو صلى على مكان طاهر إلا أنه إذا سجد تقع ثيابه على أرض

قوله : (وأراد بالمكان الخ) قال في النهر : ليس في كلامه ما يدل على اختصاص المكان بما ذكر، بل الظاهر الإطلاق فقد اختار الفقيه خلاف ظاهر الرواية وصححه في العيون وهو المناسب لإطلاق عامة المتون. وفي الخانية : وكذا لو كانت النجاسة في موضع السجود أو الركبتين أو اليدين يعني تجمع ولا يجعل كأنه لم يضع العضو على النجاسة، وهذا كما لو صلى رافعاً إحدى قدميه جازت صلاته، ولو وضع القدم على النجاسة لا تجوز ولا يجعل كأنه لم يضع .اهـ. وهو يفيد أن عدم اشتراط طهارة مكان اليدين أو الركبتين إذا لم يضعهما، أما إن وضعهما اشترطت فليحفظ هذا كذا في فتح القدير. وأقول : لو خرج ما في الخانية على رأي الفقيه لكان أظهر فتدبره .اهـ. هذا وفي منية المصلي ما نصه : ذكر شمس الأئمة السرخسي أنه إذا كانت النجاسة موضع الكفين والركبتين جازت صلاته . البحر الرائق ج ١/
And as for the purity of his garment, for His statement: 'And your garment purify' (74:4). The most apparent is that it means your worn garment, and its meaning is to purify it from impurity. It has been said otherwise, but the preponderant is what we mentioned, and it is the statement of the jurists, and it is correct as mentioned by al-Nawawi in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab, and due to the generality of the two previous hadiths. And when purification is obligatory for the garment as we mentioned, it is obligatory for the place and the body by a fortiori reasoning, because they are more binding for the worshiper than the garment, since the garment can be removed unlike them. And by khabath he means the preventing amount that he mentioned in the chapter on impurities, so the absolute does not apply. And by stipulating purity of the garment, he indicates that if he carries a preventing impurity, his prayer is invalid. So also if the impurity is on the end of his turban or his handkerchief, which is intended as a garment he is wearing, and he throws that end on the ground and prays, then if it moves with his movement it is not permissible, otherwise it is permissible, because by that movement it is attributed to carrying the impurity. In al-Zahiriyyah: If a child's garment is impure or he is impure and he sits on the lap of the worshiper while he is supporting him, or a pigeon impure if it lands on the worshiper's head... and if it is not, it would be a cause and not a medium overall, and if not, it would be a cause. And what is in Ghurar al-Adhkar that the condition of a thing is that the thing exists when it exists, not by its existence nor without it, all together.

His saying: (And he gave precedence to hadath due to its strength, because its small amount is a preventer) there is a consideration because hadath has no small amount, as it is indivisible. And it is possible that by 'its small amount' he meant a small spot (lum'a) as a leniency. And what was mentioned in Ghayat al-Bayan is not valid according to the correct view of the purity of used water, and on the view of its impurity, the answer is that what is meant by 'greater severity' is severity in terms of preventing prayer, as some scholars said. His saying: (The intended garment is what he is wearing) he inserted that in the middle of the speech to explain that the meaning is not specifically the handkerchief but general.

So end quote.

He prays likewise, the prayer is valid. And similarly, if a junub or one with hadath is carried by the worshiper, because what is on the worshiper is used by him, so the worshiper does not become a carrier of impurity. End quote. And his statement indicates that if he prays while his head reaches an impure ceiling, or in an impure mosquito net, or in an impure tent, then it is not valid because he is a carrier of impurity. For this reason he said in al-Qunyah: If he prays in a tent and raises its roof for the completeness of his standing, it is valid if it is pure; otherwise, no. And in al-Muhit: If he prays while in his hand is a rope tied to the neck of a dog, his prayer is valid, because when the rope fell to the ground, the ruling of connection was severed, so it becomes like a long turban. End quote. Similarly, if the rope is tied around its waist, and similarly if it is tied to a ship containing impurity. The school of al-Shafi'i is that prayer is not valid in these matters because he is a carrier of impurity, as al-Nawawi transmitted. And if he prays with a puppy dog or whatever it is not permissible to perform wudu with its leftover water; it is said it is not valid, and the most correct is that if its mouth is open, it is not valid because its saliva flows into his sleeve, so it becomes wet with its saliva, thus his sleeve becomes impure, preventing validity if it is more than the amount of a dirham. But if its mouth is tied such that its saliva does not reach his garment, it is valid, because the apparent of every animal is pure, and it only becomes impure by death, and its internal impurity is in its organ, so its ruling does not appear, like the impurity of the inside of the worshiper. And if he prays while in his sleeve is a closed bottle containing urine, his prayer is not valid, because it is not in its organ and place. And if he prays while in his sleeve is a rotten egg whose yolk has turned to blood, it is valid, because it is in its organ. And as long as something is in its organ, it is not given the ruling of impurity. All from al-Muhit. And he meant by place: the place of the foot and prostration only. As for the purity of the place of the foot, it is by agreement of the narrations, on condition that he places them on the impurity. But if he lifts the foot whose place is impure and prays, it is valid. And as for the purity of the place of prostration, according to the soundest of the two narrations from Abu Hanifa, and it is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And if the impurity is in the place of his hands, knees, armpits, and chest, his prayer is valid, because placing on the impurity is not a placement, and prostration on the hands and knees is not obligatory, so it is as if he did not prostrate on them. And this is the apparent narration. Abu al-Layth chose that his prayer is invalid, and it was corrected in al-Uyun. And if he prays on a pure place, but when he prostrates his clothes fall on impure ground.

His saying: (And he meant by place etc.) He said in al-Nahr: There is nothing in his speech that indicates restriction of place to what was mentioned; rather, the apparent is absolute. Al-Faqih chose contrary to the apparent narration and corrected it in al-Uyun, and it is appropriate for the absolute of most texts. And in al-Khaniyyah: Similarly, if the impurity is in the place of prostration, knees, or hands, meaning he gathers? and it is not considered as if he did not place the limb on the impurity. This is like if he prays lifting one of his feet, his prayer is valid, but if he places the foot on impurity, it is not valid and it is not considered as if he did not place. End quote. And it indicates that not requiring purity of the place of the hands or knees is when he does not place them; but if he places them, it is required. So keep this. This is in Fath al-Qadir. And I say: If the statement in al-Khaniyyah were based on the view of al-Faqih, it would be more apparent, so contemplate it. End quote. And in Munyat al-Musalli: Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentioned that if impurity is in the place of the palms and knees, his prayer is valid. Al-Bahr al-Raiq vol. 1.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the obligation of purity from physical impurity (khabath) for prayer. It begins with the Quranic proof from Surah al-Muddaththir (74:4) that one must purify his garments, which the jurists interpret literally as physical purification. By a fortiori analogy (qiyas awla), this obligation extends to the body and place of prayer. The commentator then discusses various scenarios: if the end of one's turban touches impure ground and moves with the worshiper, the prayer is invalid because he is considered to be carrying the impurity. If it does not move, it is permissible. He then discusses a child with impure clothes sitting on the worshiper's lap: the prayer is valid because the child is not being carried by the worshiper as a load? Actually the text says if the child is on his lap and he is supporting him, the prayer is valid. Similarly, carrying a junub or one with hadath does not invalidate prayer. Then he discusses a rope tied to a dog: if the rope falls to the ground, the connection is severed and prayer is valid. The Shafi'i school holds the opposite view. For a dog with an open mouth, the saliva may wet the sleeve and invalidate prayer if the amount of impurity exceeds a dirham. For a bottle of urine in the sleeve, prayer is invalid because the urine is not in its original place (the bladder). For a rotten egg whose yolk has turned to blood, prayer is valid because it is in its original place. He then discusses the place of prayer: the place of the feet and prostration must be pure. For the feet, if they are placed on impurity, prayer is invalid; if lifted, it is valid. For prostration, the soundest narration from Abu Hanifa, and the view of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), is that the place of prostration must be pure. For the hands, knees, etc., the apparent narration is that impurity in those places does not invalidate, because placing them is not obligatory. However, Abu al-Layth chose invalidation. The commentator then cites al-Nahr and al-Khaniyyah, and concludes that if one does not place the hands/knees on impurity, prayer is valid, but if he places them, it is invalid. He ends by quoting Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi that prayer is valid even if impurity is under the palms and knees.

Key Terms
مستعمل له (musta'mal lahu): A person or thing that is being used by the worshiper (e.g., a child on the lap) so that the worshiper is not considered to be carrying its impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key factor that determines whether prayer is valid when impurity is on an attached object (e.g., end of a turban)?
A) The amount of impurity (whether it is more than a dirham).
B) Whether the impurity moves with the worshiper's movement.
C) Whether the object is considered a garment or not.
D) Whether the impurity is wet or dry.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school determines invalidity by whether the impurity moves with the worshiper (connection), not merely by its presence in the vicinity.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
12 min
The Principle of Ma'din and Purity of the Place of Prayer
Ma'din principle and purity of prostration area

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi concept of ma'din (original place) to cases of impurity in containers and evaluate the dispute over impurity under hands and knees during prostration.

Core Idea
Impurity in its original place (ma'din) is not considered impure for contact; placing hands/knees on impurity is not invalid because it is not obligatory to place them.

This lesson covers two related topics: the ma'din principle (impurity in its source, e.g., urine in the bladder, blood in the body, yolk in the egg) and the purity of the place for prostration. The Hanafi school distinguishes between impurity that is in its natural container (ma'din) and impurity that has exited. Additionally, the dispute over whether impurity under the hands and knees invalidates prayer is settled by the principal that these body parts are not wajib to place during prostration.

Guided Path
1
Define the ma'din principle
Read the cases: a closed bottle of urine (invalid) vs. a rotten egg with blood (valid). The key is whether the impurity is in its original place.
Urine is no longer in the bladder; the egg's content is originally in the egg.
2
Apply the ma'din principle
If something is still in its ma'din (e.g., blood in a vein, fluid inside an intact egg), it is not considered outwardly impure.
The ruling changes once it exits the ma'din.
3
Examine purity of the place of prayer
The text specifies that only the place of the feet and prostration must be pure. Hands, knees, armpits etc. are not required to be pure because placing them is not obligatory.
This is the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya).
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu al-Layth and some others hold that impurity under hands/knees invalidates, by analogy to the feet.
The author sides with the apparent narration, and cites al-Sarakhsi.
5
Derive the practical ruling
If the worshiper does not place his hands/knees on impurity (e.g., lifts them), prayer is valid. If he places them, according to some, it is invalid; according to the relied-upon view, it is valid.
The text from al-Khaniyyah indicates that if he places them, purity is required.

وأما طهارة ثوبه فلقوله تعالى وثيابك فطهر ) [المدثر : ٤] فإن الأظهر أن المراد ثيابك الملبوسة وأن معناه طهرها من النجاسة. وقد قيل في الآية غير هذا لكن الأرجح ما ذكرناه وهو قول الفقهاء وهو الصحيح كما ذكره النووي في شرح المهذب، ولعموم الحديثين السابقين. وإذا وجب التطهير لما ذكرناه في الثوب وجب في المكان والبدن بالأولى لأنهما ألزم للمصلي منه لتصور انفصاله بخلافهما وأراد بالخبث القدر المانع الذي قدمه في باب الأنجاس فلا يرد عليه الإطلاق. وأشار باشتراط طهارة الثوب إلى أنه لو حمل نجاسة مانعة فإن صلاته باطلة. فكذا لو كانت النجاسة في طرف عمامته أو منديله المقصود ثوب هو لابسه فألقى ذلك الطرف على الأرض وصلى فإنه إن تحرك بحركته لا يجوز وإلا يجوز، لأنه بتلك الحركة ينسب لحمل النجاسة. وفي الظهيرية : الصبي إذا كان ثوبه نجساً أو هو نجس فجلس على حجر المصلي وهو يستمسك، أو الحمام النجس إذا وقع على رأس المصلي وهو وإلا كان علة وغير موصل في الجملة وإلا كان سبباً. وما في غرر الأذكار من أن شرط الشيء ما يوجد ذلك الشيء عند وجوده لا بوجوده ولا بدونه أجمع.

قوله: (وقدم الحدث لقوته لأن قليله مانع (الخ فيه نظر لأن الحدث لا قليل له لأنه لا يتجزأ، ويمكن أن يراد بقليله اللمعة تساهلاً، وما أورده في غاية البيان غير وارد على الصحيح من طهارة المستعمل، وعلى القول بنجاسته يجاب بأن المراد بالأغلظية الأغلظية من حيث منع الصلاة قاله بعض الفضلاء. قوله : (المقصود ثوب هو لابسه أقحم ذلك في أثناء الكلام لبيان أن المراد ليس خصوص المنديل بل أعم.

And as for the purity of his garment, for His statement: 'And your garment purify' (74:4). The most apparent is that it means your worn garment, and its meaning is to purify it from impurity. It has been said otherwise, but the preponderant is what we mentioned, and it is the statement of the jurists, and it is correct as mentioned by al-Nawawi in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab, and due to the generality of the two previous hadiths. He prays likewise, the prayer is valid. And similarly, if a junub or one with hadath is carried by the worshiper, because what is on the worshiper is used by him, so the worshiper does not become a carrier of impurity. End quote. And his statement indicates that if he prays while his head reaches an impure ceiling, or in an impure mosquito net, or in an impure tent, then it is not valid because he is a carrier of impurity. For this reason he said in al-Qunyah: If he prays in a tent and raises its roof for the completeness of his standing, it is valid if it is pure; otherwise, no. And in al-Muhit: If he prays while in his hand is a rope tied to the neck of a dog, his prayer is valid, because when the rope fell to the ground, the ruling of connection was severed, so it becomes like a long turban. End quote. Similarly, if the rope is tied around its waist, and similarly if it is tied to a ship containing impurity. The school of al-Shafi'i is that prayer is not valid in these matters because he is a carrier of impurity, as al-Nawawi transmitted. And if he prays with a puppy dog or whatever it is not permissible to perform wudu with its leftover water; it is said it is not valid, and the most correct is that if its mouth is open, it is not valid because its saliva flows into his sleeve, so it becomes wet with its saliva, thus his sleeve becomes impure, preventing validity if it is more than the amount of a dirham. But if its mouth is tied such that its saliva does not reach his garment, it is valid, because the apparent of every animal is pure, and it only becomes impure by death, and its internal impurity is in its organ, so its ruling does not appear, like the impurity of the inside of the worshiper. And if he prays while in his sleeve is a closed bottle containing urine, his prayer is not valid, because it is not in its organ and place. And if he prays while in his sleeve is a rotten egg whose yolk has turned to blood, it is valid, because it is in its organ. And as long as something is in its organ, it is not given the ruling of impurity. All from al-Muhit. And he meant by place: the place of the foot and prostration only. As for the purity of the place of the foot, it is by agreement of the narrations, on condition that he places them on the impurity. But if he lifts the foot whose place is impure and prays, it is valid. And as for the purity of the place of prostration, according to the soundest of the two narrations from Abu Hanifa, and it is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And if the impurity is in the place of his hands, knees, armpits, and chest, his prayer is valid, because placing on the impurity is not a placement, and prostration on the hands and knees is not obligatory, so it is as if he did not prostrate on them. And this is the apparent narration. Abu al-Layth chose that his prayer is invalid, and it was corrected in al-Uyun. And if he prays on a pure place, but when he prostrates his clothes fall on impure ground.

His saying: (And he meant by place etc.) He said in al-Nahr: There is nothing in his speech that indicates restriction of place to what was mentioned; rather, the apparent is absolute. Al-Faqih chose contrary to the apparent narration and corrected it in al-Uyun, and it is appropriate for the absolute of most texts. And in al-Khaniyyah: Similarly, if the impurity is in the place of prostration, knees, or hands, meaning he gathers? and it is not considered as if he did not place the limb on the impurity. This is like if he prays lifting one of his feet, his prayer is valid, but if he places the foot on impurity, it is not valid and it is not considered as if he did not place. End quote. And it indicates that not requiring purity of the place of the hands or knees is when he does not place them; but if he places them, it is required. So keep this. This is in Fath al-Qadir. And I say: If the statement in al-Khaniyyah were based on the view of al-Faqih, it would be more apparent, so contemplate it. End quote. And in Munyat al-Musalli: Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentioned that if impurity is in the place of the palms and knees, his prayer is valid. Al-Bahr al-Raiq vol. 1.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the obligation of purity from physical impurity (khabath) for prayer. It begins with the Quranic proof from Surah al-Muddaththir (74:4) that one must purify his garments, which the jurists interpret literally as physical purification. By a fortiori analogy (qiyas awla), this obligation extends to the body and place of prayer. The commentator then discusses various scenarios: if the end of one's turban touches impure ground and moves with the worshiper, the prayer is invalid because he is considered to be carrying the impurity. If it does not move, it is permissible. He then discusses a child with impure clothes sitting on the worshiper's lap: the prayer is valid because the child is not being carried by the worshiper as a load? Actually the text says if the child is on his lap and he is supporting him, the prayer is valid. Similarly, carrying a junub or one with hadath does not invalidate prayer. Then he discusses a rope tied to a dog: if the rope falls to the ground, the connection is severed and prayer is valid. The Shafi'i school holds the opposite view. For a dog with an open mouth, the saliva may wet the sleeve and invalidate prayer if the amount of impurity exceeds a dirham. For a bottle of urine in the sleeve, prayer is invalid because the urine is not in its original place (the bladder). For a rotten egg whose yolk has turned to blood, prayer is valid because it is in its original place. He then discusses the place of prayer: the place of the feet and prostration must be pure. For the feet, if they are placed on impurity, prayer is invalid; if lifted, it is valid. For prostration, the soundest narration from Abu Hanifa, and the view of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), is that the place of prostration must be pure. For the hands, knees, etc., the apparent narration is that impurity in those places does not invalidate, because placing them is not obligatory. However, Abu al-Layth chose invalidation. The commentator then cites al-Nahr and al-Khaniyyah, and concludes that if one does not place the hands/knees on impurity, prayer is valid, but if he places them, it is invalid. He ends by quoting Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi that prayer is valid even if impurity is under the palms and knees.

Key Terms
معدن (ma'din): The original place of something (e.g., urine in the bladder, blood in the body, yolk in the egg).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for impurity on the place of the hands during prostration in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya)?
A) Prayer is invalid because hands are in contact with impurity.
B) Prayer is valid because placing hands is not obligatory.
C) Prayer is invalid by analogy to the feet.
D) Prayer is valid if the impurity is less than a dirham.

Key Takeaway
The ma'din principle keeps internal impurities from being considered contaminating, and the Hanafi school does not require purity for body parts whose placement is not obligatory (hands, knees) during prostration.

Carrying Impurity During Prayer: General Rules and the Dog Rope Dispute
Rulings on general cases of carrying impurity and the dog rope ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prayer validity when carrying various impurities and explain the Shafi'i dispute on the dog rope case.

Core Idea
Carrying external impurity invalidates prayer unless the connection is severed.

This lesson examines several scenarios where a person might be carrying an impurity during salat: carrying a junub, praying under a najis ceiling or tent, and holding a rope tied to a dog. The Hanafi rulings hinge on whether the person is considered 'hamil' (carrier) of the impurity, and the rope-on-ground case illustrates a crucial exception based on physical disconnection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the impurity
For each case, clearly state the act (carrying, praying under) and the specific impurity involved (junub person, najis ceiling, dog).
The junub case: the impurity is on the carried person, not transferred.
2
Apply the principle of carrier (hamil)
Determine if the praying person is directly carrying the impurity. In the junub case, the impurity is 'used' by the carried person, so the carrier is not considered impure.
Key distinction: carrying a junub vs. carrying najas itself.
3
Analyze the connection for the rope case
When the rope falls to the ground, the ruling of connection is severed. The rope becomes like an extension of the ground, not part of the person.
Contrast with rope still taut off the ground (not covered here).
4
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that any attachment to impurity invalidates prayer, as per al-Nawawi's transmission.
This ikhtilaf is a legitimate difference in usul: Hanafis consider physical disconnection sufficient.
5
State the final Hanafi ruling
For the dog rope: prayer valid. For najis ceiling/tent: prayer invalid. For carrying junub: prayer valid.
The ruling for tent is conditional on purity; if tent is impure, prayer invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يصلي كذلك جازت الصلاة، وكذلك الجنب أو المحدث إذا حمله المصلي لأن الذي على المصلي مستعمل له فلم يصر المصلي حاملاً للنجاسة ا.هـ. ودل كلامه أنه لو صلى ورأسه يصل إلى السقف النجس أو في كلة متنجسة أو في خيمة كذلك فإنها لا تصح لكونه حاملاً للنجاسة، ولهذا قال في القنية : إذا صلى في الخيمة ورفع سقفها لتمام قيامه جاز إذا كانت طاهرة وإلا وفي المحيط : لو صلى وفي يده حبل مشدود على عنق الكلب تجوز صلاته لأن الحبل لما سقط على الأرض فقد انقطع حكم الاتصال به فصار كالعمامة الطويلة ا هـ. وكذا لو كان الحبل مشدوداً في وسطه، وكذا لو كان مربوطاً في سفينة فيها نجاسة، ومذهب الشافعي أن الصلاة لا تصح في هذه المسائل لأنه حامل للنجاسة كما نقله النووي ولو صلى ومعه جرو كلب أو كل ما لا يجوز أن يتوضأ بسؤره ؛ قيل لم يجز والأصح أنه إن كان فمه مفتوحاً لم يجز لأن لعابه يسيل في كمه فيصير مبتلاً بلعابه فيتنجس كمه فيمنع الجواز إن كان أكثر من قدر الدرهم وإن كان فمه مشدوداً بحيث لا يصل لعابه إلى ثوبه جاز لأن ظاهر كل حيوان طاهر، ولا ينجس إلا بالموت ونجاسة باطنه في معدنه فلا يظهر حكمها كنجاسة باطن المصلي. ولو صلى وفي كمه قارورة مضمومة فيها بول لم تجز صلاته لأنه في غير معدنه ومكانه، ولو صلى وفي كمه بيضة مذرة قد صار محها دماً جازت لأنه في معدنه، والشيء ما دام في معدنه لا يعطي له حكم النجاسة. الكل في المحيط.
If he prays in this manner, his prayer is valid. Similarly, if a junub or one in hadath is carried by the one praying, because what is on the carrier is used by him, so the praying person does not become a carrier of impurity. End. His statement indicates that if he prays with his head touching a najis ceiling, or in a najis tent, or in a covered enclosure likewise, then it is not valid because he is carrying the impurity. For this reason, it is stated in al-Qunyah: If he prays in a tent and lifts its roof for the completion of his standing, it is valid if it is pure, otherwise not. If its mouth is tied such that its saliva does not reach his clothes, it is valid because the external part of every animal is pure; it only becomes impure by death, and the impurity of its interior is in its place of origin (ma'din), so its ruling does not appear, like the interior impurity of the praying person. If he prays with a decaying egg whose yolk has turned to blood, it is valid because it is in its place of origin. Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings on the effect of carrying impurity during prayer, drawn from al-Muhit and other sources. The overarching principle is that the prayer is invalid if the person is actually carrying a physical impurity that is external to his body and not in its natural place. The text distinguishes cases where the impurity is considered 'carried' (hamil) from those where it is not. For example, carrying a junub person does not make the carrier impure because the impurity is on the carrier and used by him, not transferred. Similarly, a rope tied to a dog that falls to the ground severs the connection, so the prayer is valid. The Shafi'i position is noted as opposing, considering any attachment to impurity invalidates. A key distinction is made regarding animals: the external part of a living animal is pure, so a puppy with closed mouth does not invalidate, but if open, saliva spreads and may invalidate if it exceeds a dirham. Items like a sealed bottle of urine invalidate because the impurity is outside its natural place, while a decaying egg (where blood is inside) is valid because the impurity remains in its place of origin. The rule that a thing in its place of origin is not treated as impure is fundamental.

Key Terms
حمل النجاسة (haml al-najasah): Carrying a physical impurity on one's person or clothing during prayer, which can invalidate it if the impurity is external and not in its ma'din.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person prays while holding a rope tied to a dog's neck, and the rope is lying on the ground, what is the ruling?
A) Prayer is invalid because the dog is impure.
B) Prayer is valid because the rope on the ground severs the connection.
C) Prayer is invalid because the person is still holding the rope.
D) Prayer is valid because the dog is not a pig.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a person is only considered to be carrying impurity if there is a direct physical connection not severed by the ground; the dog rope case illustrates this with a valid prayer.

Ruling on Praying with a Puppy: The Role of Saliva
Ruling on puppy saliva during prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the validity of prayer when carrying a puppy based on whether its mouth is open and the amount of saliva transferred.

Core Idea
A puppy's external body is pure; its saliva only invalidates prayer if it wets the sleeve and exceeds a dirham.

This lesson addresses the specific case of praying while carrying a puppy or any animal whose saliva is not permitted for wudu. The Hanafi ruling distinguishes between the animal's mouth being open or closed, and emphasizes that the external part of a living animal is inherently pure. Understanding this nuance helps avoid unnecessary invalidation of prayers when interacting with animals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the animal and its saliva ruling
Determine if the animal's saliva is considered impure (i.e., not allowed for wudu). Dogs, for example, have su'r that is not permissible.
The text mentions 'كل ما لا يجوز أن يتوضأ بسؤره' – animals whose leftover water is not allowed.
2
Check the mouth of the animal
Is the mouth open or tied/stuck closed? If open, saliva may flow; if closed, it likely does not reach the clothing.
The ruling depends on whether the saliva can physically transfer.
3
Assess wetness on clothing
If the mouth is open and saliva wets the sleeve, the wet area becomes impure. Measure the area: if it exceeds the size of a dirham (approx. 4.4 cm diameter), the prayer is invalid.
The dirham threshold is standard for assessing najasa on clothing.
4
Apply the principle of external purity
Recall that the external body of a living animal (fur, skin) is pure. The impurity of the saliva is from inside, but once it exits and contacts clothing, it becomes external impurity.
This is why closed mouth (no saliva exit) keeps the prayer valid.
5
State the final ruling
If mouth open and saliva wets sleeve > dirham: prayer invalid. If mouth closed or no wetness: prayer valid.
The text says 'الأصح' (the most correct) so this is the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يصلي كذلك جازت الصلاة، وكذلك الجنب أو المحدث إذا حمله المصلي لأن الذي على المصلي مستعمل له فلم يصر المصلي حاملاً للنجاسة ا.هـ. ودل كلامه أنه لو صلى ورأسه يصل إلى السقف النجس أو في كلة متنجسة أو في خيمة كذلك فإنها لا تصح لكونه حاملاً للنجاسة، ولهذا قال في القنية : إذا صلى في الخيمة ورفع سقفها لتمام قيامه جاز إذا كانت طاهرة وإلا وفي المحيط : لو صلى وفي يده حبل مشدود على عنق الكلب تجوز صلاته لأن الحبل لما سقط على الأرض فقد انقطع حكم الاتصال به فصار كالعمامة الطويلة ا هـ. وكذا لو كان الحبل مشدوداً في وسطه، وكذا لو كان مربوطاً في سفينة فيها نجاسة، ومذهب الشافعي أن الصلاة لا تصح في هذه المسائل لأنه حامل للنجاسة كما نقله النووي ولو صلى ومعه جرو كلب أو كل ما لا يجوز أن يتوضأ بسؤره ؛ قيل لم يجز والأصح أنه إن كان فمه مفتوحاً لم يجز لأن لعابه يسيل في كمه فيصير مبتلاً بلعابه فيتنجس كمه فيمنع الجواز إن كان أكثر من قدر الدرهم وإن كان فمه مشدوداً بحيث لا يصل لعابه إلى ثوبه جاز لأن ظاهر كل حيوان طاهر، ولا ينجس إلا بالموت ونجاسة باطنه في معدنه فلا يظهر حكمها كنجاسة باطن المصلي. ولو صلى وفي كمه قارورة مضمومة فيها بول لم تجز صلاته لأنه في غير معدنه ومكانه، ولو صلى وفي كمه بيضة مذرة قد صار محها دماً جازت لأنه في معدنه، والشيء ما دام في معدنه لا يعطي له حكم النجاسة. الكل في المحيط.

The Ma'din Principle: Impurity in Its Natural Place
Rulings on ma'din principle with bottle and egg examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of ma'din (place of origin) to determine whether carrying an impurity affects prayer validity.

Core Idea
An impurity in its natural place (ma'din) does not affect prayer; only external impurity outside its ma'din invalidates.

This lesson focuses on the concept of ma'din—the natural reservoir where an impurity originates. Using the examples of a sealed bottle of urine and a decaying egg, we learn that as long as the impurity remains within its original location (like blood inside an egg or urine inside the bladder), it is not considered 'carried' impurity that invalidates prayer. This principle has broad applications in taharah.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between impurity in its ma'din vs. outside it
Identify whether the impurity is in its natural place of origin (e.g., blood inside an egg, urine inside the bladder) or has been removed (e.g., urine in a bottle).
The text gives two contrasting examples: egg (ma'din) vs. bottle (not ma'din).
2
Apply the rule to the sealed bottle case
Urine in a sealed bottle inside the sleeve: the urine is not in its ma'din (the bladder). Therefore, the person is carrying external impurity, and prayer is invalid.
The bottle is sealed, but the impurity is still external because it is not in the body.
3
Apply the rule to the decaying egg
The egg's contents have turned to blood, but that blood is still inside the egg (its natural container). The egg is considered the ma'din for that blood. Therefore, the person is not carrying external impurity.
'محها دماً' – the yolk becomes blood, but it is still within the egg.
4
Understand the broader principle
A thing as long as it remains in its ma'din does not take the ruling of najasa. This is analogous to impurities inside the human body (like blood in veins) not affecting prayer.
This principle also applies to animals (saliva inside mouth is in its ma'din) and other contexts.
5
Conclude the prayer ruling
Sealed bottle of urine: prayer invalid. Decaying egg: prayer valid.
The key is whether the impurity has left its natural origin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يصلي كذلك جازت الصلاة، وكذلك الجنب أو المحدث إذا حمله المصلي لأن الذي على المصلي مستعمل له فلم يصر المصلي حاملاً للنجاسة ا.هـ. ودل كلامه أنه لو صلى ورأسه يصل إلى السقف النجس أو في كلة متنجسة أو في خيمة كذلك فإنها لا تصح لكونه حاملاً للنجاسة، ولهذا قال في القنية : إذا صلى في الخيمة ورفع سقفها لتمام قيامه جاز إذا كانت طاهرة وإلا وفي المحيط : لو صلى وفي يده حبل مشدود على عنق الكلب تجوز صلاته لأن الحبل لما سقط على الأرض فقد انقطع حكم الاتصال به فصار كالعمامة الطويلة ا هـ. وكذا لو كان الحبل مشدوداً في وسطه، وكذا لو كان مربوطاً في سفينة فيها نجاسة، ومذهب الشافعي أن الصلاة لا تصح في هذه المسائل لأنه حامل للنجاسة كما نقله النووي ولو صلى ومعه جرو كلب أو كل ما لا يجوز أن يتوضأ بسؤره ؛ قيل لم يجز والأصح أنه إن كان فمه مفتوحاً لم يجز لأن لعابه يسيل في كمه فيصير مبتلاً بلعابه فيتنجس كمه فيمنع الجواز إن كان أكثر من قدر الدرهم وإن كان فمه مشدوداً بحيث لا يصل لعابه إلى ثوبه جاز لأن ظاهر كل حيوان طاهر، ولا ينجس إلا بالموت ونجاسة باطنه في معدنه فلا يظهر حكمها كنجاسة باطن المصلي. ولو صلى وفي كمه قارورة مضمومة فيها بول لم تجز صلاته لأنه في غير معدنه ومكانه، ولو صلى وفي كمه بيضة مذرة قد صار محها دماً جازت لأنه في معدنه، والشيء ما دام في معدنه لا يعطي له حكم النجاسة. الكل في المحيط.
If he prays in this manner, his prayer is valid. Similarly, if a junub or one in hadath is carried by the one praying, because what is on the carrier is used by him, so the praying person does not become a carrier of impurity. End. His statement indicates that if he prays with his head touching a najis ceiling, or in a najis tent, or in a covered enclosure likewise, then it is not valid because he is carrying the impurity. For this reason, it is stated in al-Qunyah: If he prays in a tent and lifts its roof for the completion of his standing, it is valid if it is pure, otherwise not. And in al-Muhit: If he prays while holding a rope tied around a dog's neck, his prayer is valid because when the rope falls to the ground, the ruling of connection is severed, so it becomes like a long turban. Similarly, if the rope is tied around the dog's waist, and similarly if it is tied to a boat containing impurity. The Shafi'i school holds that prayer is not valid in these cases because he is carrying impurity, as al-Nawawi transmitted. And if he prays with a puppy or anything whose saliva is not permissible for wudu, it is said it is not permissible, and the correct view is that if its mouth is open, it is not permissible because its saliva flows into his sleeve, making his sleeve wet with its saliva and thus impure, preventing validity if it is more than the size of a dirham. If its mouth is tied such that its saliva does not reach his clothes, it is valid because the external part of every animal is pure; it only becomes impure by death, and the impurity of its interior is in its place of origin (ma'din), so its ruling does not appear, like the interior impurity of the praying person. If he prays with a sealed bottle containing urine in his sleeve, his prayer is not valid because it is not in its place of origin. If he prays with a decaying egg whose yolk has turned to blood, it is valid because it is in its place of origin. A thing as long as it is in its place of origin, no ruling of impurity is given to it. All from al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings on the effect of carrying impurity during prayer, drawn from al-Muhit and other sources. The overarching principle is that the prayer is invalid if the person is actually carrying a physical impurity that is external to his body and not in its natural place. The text distinguishes cases where the impurity is considered 'carried' (hamil) from those where it is not. For example, carrying a junub person does not make the carrier impure because the impurity is on the carrier and used by him, not transferred. Similarly, a rope tied to a dog that falls to the ground severs the connection, so the prayer is valid. The Shafi'i position is noted as opposing, considering any attachment to impurity invalidates. A key distinction is made regarding animals: the external part of a living animal is pure, so a puppy with closed mouth does not invalidate, but if open, saliva spreads and may invalidate if it exceeds a dirham. Items like a sealed bottle of urine invalidate because the impurity is outside its natural place, while a decaying egg (where blood is inside) is valid because the impurity remains in its place of origin. The rule that a thing in its place of origin is not treated as impure is fundamental.

Key Terms
معدن (ma'din): The natural place or origin of a thing; impurities that remain within their ma'din (e.g., blood inside an egg, or impurities inside the body) do not affect the prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is prayer valid when carrying a decaying egg with internal blood but invalid when carrying a sealed bottle of urine?
A) Because the egg is smaller than the bottle.
B) Because the blood is in its ma'din (the egg), while the urine is not in its ma'din (the bladder).
C) Because urine is more impure than blood.
D) Because the egg is sealed in its shell.

Key Takeaway
The ma'din principle provides a clear distinction: internal impurities do not affect prayer; external impurities outside their origin do.

Taharah — Purification
